Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke - Post-discharge care You were admitted to the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke occurs when the blood flow to a part of the brain stops. At home, follow your healthcare provider’s self-care instructions. The information below is to help you remember. While you were in the hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain and to help your heart, lungs, and other important organs recover. After your condition became stable, the medical staff performed tests and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and to prevent future strokes. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps with recovery after a stroke. What to expect at home Because of brain damage from the stroke, you may feel the following problems: - Changes in behavior - Ability to do simple tasks - Memory - Moving one side of the body - Muscle spasms - Concentration - Sensation or awareness of a part of the body - Swallowing - Speaking or understanding what others say - Thinking - Visual disturbance where you can see only one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may receive physical therapy or occupational therapy to help you regain these functions. After a stroke, depression is relatively common as you go through the process of adapting to changes. This can occur at any time from right after the stroke up to as long as 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive a car without your healthcare provider’s permission. Moving After a stroke, it may be difficult to move or to do the things you used to do. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your healthcare provider, therapist, or nurse how you can change your home to make daily activities easier. Learn how to prevent falls and how to use the bathroom safely. Family and caregivers may need to help with the following: - Exercises to prevent the elbows, shoulders, and other joints from becoming stiff - Watching...

After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Stroke - Discharge You were admitted to the hospital for a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home, follow your medical team's self-care instructions. Use the information below as a reminder. **When You Were in the Hospital** First, you received treatment to stop further damage to the brain and help the heart, lungs, and other vital organs heal. After your condition stabilized, the medical team performed tests and started treatment for stroke recovery and prevention of future strokes. You may have stayed in a special unit (stroke intensive care unit) that helps recovery after a stroke. **What to Expect at Home** Because of possible brain damage from the stroke, you may experience the following problems: * Behavior changes * Doing simple tasks * Memory * Moving one side of the body * Muscle spasms * Paying attention * Sensation or awareness of body parts * Swallowing * Speaking or understanding others * Thinking skills (thinking) * Seeing only one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you did alone before the stroke. You may receive physical therapy or occupational therapy to recover the ability to perform these functions. Depression after stroke is quite common as you adjust to a changed life. It can occur immediately after the stroke or up to 2 years later. **Do not drive without your medical team's permission.** **Moving Around** After a stroke, moving or doing normal tasks can be difficult. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your medical team, therapist, or nurse about changing your home environment to make daily activities easier. Learn how to prevent falls and use the bathroom safely. Family and caregivers may need to help with the following: * Exercises to keep elbows, shoulders, and other joints loose * Watching for joint stiffening (contracture) * Checking that splints are used correctly * Checking that arms and legs are in a good position when sitting or lyin...

Item: EVAL_0259 | Document: SURG_001 | Model: gpt-5.1

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. 외과적 상처 관리 - 열린 상처 절개(incision)는 수술 중에 피부를 가르는 절단입니다. 이를 수술 상처(surgical wound)라고도 합니다. 어떤 절개는 작고, 어떤 절개는 깁니다. 절개의 크기는 받은 수술의 종류에 따라 달라집니다. 때때로 절개 부위가 벌어질 수 있습니다. 이는 절개 전체가 벌어질 수도 있고, 일부만 벌어질 수도 있습니다. 이런 경우, 담당 외과의사는 다시 봉합사(sutures, stitches)로 상처를 닫지 않기로 결정할 수 있습니다. 집에서 기대할 수 있는 것 외과의사가 상처를 다시 봉합사로 닫지 않기로 했다면, 상처가 치유되는 데 시간이 걸릴 수 있으므로 집에서 상처를 관리해야 합니다. 상처는 아래쪽부터 위쪽 방향으로 치유됩니다. 드레싱(dressing)은 분비물을 흡수하고, 상처 안쪽이 채워지기 전에 피부가 먼저 닫히는 것을 막는 데 도움이 됩니다. 올바른 손 씻기 드레싱을 갈기 전에 손을 깨끗이 씻는 것이 중요합니다. 알코올 성분의 손 소독제를 사용할 수 있습니다. 또는 다음 단계에 따라 손을 씻을 수 있습니다. 손에 낀 모든 장신구를 제거합니다. 따뜻한 흐르는 물 아래에서 손을 아래로 향하게 하고 적십니다. 비누를 묻혀 15~30초 동안 손을 문질러 씻습니다(“생일 축하 노래”나 “알파벳 노래”를 한 번 부르는 시간 정도). 손톱 밑도 깨끗이 닦습니다. 잘 헹굽니다. 깨끗한 수건으로 말립니다. 오래된 드레싱 제거하기 의료 제공자(의사 또는 간호사)는 드레싱을 얼마나 자주 갈아야 하는지 알려줄 것입니다. 드레싱 교체를 준비할 때는 다음을 따르십시오. 드레싱을 만지기 전에 손을 깨끗이 씻습니다. 필요한 모든 물품이 가까이에 있는지 확인합니다. 깨끗한 작업 공간을 준비합니다. 오래된 드레싱을 제거합니다. 피부에서 테이프를 조심스럽게 떼어냅니다. 깨끗한(멸균은 아님) 의료용 장갑을 끼고, 오래된 드레싱을 잡아당겨 제거합니다. 드레싱이 상처에 달라붙어 있으면, 물을 적셔 다시 시도합니다. 단, 의료 제공자가 마른 상태로 떼어내라고 지시한 경우는 예외입니다. 오래된 드레싱은 비닐봉지에 넣어 따로 둡니다. 오래된 드레싱을 제거한 후 다시 손을 씻습니다. 상처 관리하기 상처 주변 피부를 닦기 위해 거즈 패드나 부드러운 천을 사용할 수 있습니다. 생리식염수(normal saline solution, 소금물)나 순한 비눗물을 사용합니다. 거즈나 천을 생리식염수나 비눗물에 적신 뒤, 상처 주변 피부를 부드럽게 톡톡 두드리거나 닦습니다. 피부에 고여 있는 모든 분비물, 마른 피, 또는 다른 찌꺼기를 제거하도록 합니다. 피부 세정제, 알코올, 과산화수소(peroxide), 요오드(iodine), 항균 성분이 들어 있는 비누는 사용하지 마십시오. 이러한 물질은 상처 조직을 손상시키고 치유를 늦출 수 있습니다. 의료 제공자가 상처를 세척(irrigate, wash out)하라고 지시할 수도 있습니다. 의료 제공자가 권장한 대로 주사기(syringe)에 생리식염수나 비눗물을 채웁니다. 주사기를 상처에서 1~6인치(2.5~15센티미터) 떨어진 곳에서 잡습니다. 분비물과 배출물을 씻어낼 수 있을 정도의 힘으로 상처 안으로 분사합니다. 깨끗하고 부드러운 마른 천이나 거즈 조각을 사용해 상처를 조심스럽게 두드려 말립니다. 의료 제공자가 괜찮다고 말하지 않은 이상, 상처 부위나 주변에 로션, 크림, 한방·허브 제품을 바르지 마십시오. 새 드레싱 대기 의료 제공자가 가르쳐 준 대로 깨끗한 드레싱을 상처 위에 올립니다. 드레싱을 적용하기 전에 적셔서 사용하는 습윤-건조(wet-to-dry) 드레싱을 사용하고 있을 수도 있습니다. 끝난 후에는 손을 씻습니다. 오래된 드레싱과 사용한 물품은 방수 비닐봉지에 버립니다. 봉지를 단단히 묶은 뒤, 한 번 더 봉지에 넣어 이중 포장하여 일반 쓰레기에 버립니다. 드레싱 교체 중에 더러워진 세탁물은 다른 빨래와 분리해서 세탁합니다. 세탁 시 표백제(bleach)를 넣어야 하는지 의료 제공자에게 문의하십시오. 드레싱은 한 번만 사용하십시오. 절대 ...

Surgical Wound Care – Open Wound An incision is a cut that opens the skin during surgery. This is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, and some incisions are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery you had. Sometimes the incision site can open up. This may be the entire incision or only part of it. In such cases, the surgeon in charge may decide not to close the wound again with sutures (stitches). What to expect at home If the surgeon decided not to close the wound again with sutures, you will need to care for the wound at home because it may take time for the wound to heal. The wound heals from the bottom toward the top. The dressing helps absorb drainage and helps prevent the skin from closing before the inside of the wound has filled in. Proper handwashing It is important to wash your hands well before changing the dressing. You may use an alcohol-based hand sanitizer. Or you may wash your hands by following these steps: Remove all jewelry from your hands. Wet your hands under warm running water with your hands pointing down. Apply soap and rub your hands together for 15–30 seconds (about the time it takes to sing the “Happy Birthday” song or the “Alphabet Song” once). Clean well under your fingernails. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the old dressing Your health care provider (doctor or nurse) will tell you how often to change the dressing. When you are getting ready to change the dressing, follow these steps: Wash your hands well before touching the dressing. Make sure all the supplies you need are close by. Prepare a clean work area. Remove the old dressing. Carefully remove the tape from your skin. Put on clean (not sterile) medical gloves and gently pull off the old dressing. If the dressing is stuck to the wound, moisten it with water and try again. However, this does not apply if your health care provider instructed you to remove it dry. 수술 상처 관리 - 개방성 상처 절개란 수술 중 피부를 자르는 것을 말합니다. 수술 상처라고도 합니다. 어떤 절개는 작고, 어떤 절개는 깁니다. 절개의 크기는 받은 수술의 종류에 따라 다릅니다. 때때로 절개 부위가 벌어지는 경우가 있습니다. 이는 절개 전체 또는 일부분에서 발생할 수 있습니다. 담당 외과의는 봉합사(실)로 다시 봉합하지 않기로 결정할 수 있습니다. 가정에서의 관리 담당 외과의가 상처를 봉합사로 다시 봉합하지 않은 경우, 치유에 시간이 걸릴 수 있으므로 가정에서 관리해야 합니다. 상처는 아래에서 위로 치유됩니다. 드레싱은 분비물을 흡수하고, 아래 상처가 채워지기 전에 피부가 닫히는 것을 방지하는 역할을 합니다. 올바른 손 씻기 드레싱을 교체하기 전에 손을 깨끗이 씻는 것이 중요합니다. 알코올 성분의 손 세정제를 사용할 수 있습니다. 또는 다음 단계에 따라 손을 씻을 수 있습니다: 손에서 모든 장신구를 뺍니다. 따뜻한 흐르는 물 아래에서 손을 아래로 향하게 하여 적십니다. 비누를 묻히고 15~30초 동안 손을 씻습니다("생일 축하합니다" 또는 "ABC 노래"를 한 번 부르는 정도). 손톱 밑도 깨끗이 씻습니다. 깨끗이 헹굽니다. 깨끗한 수건으로 닦습니다. 오래된 드레싱 제거하기 담당 의료진이 드레싱을 얼마나 자주 교체해야 하는지 알려줄 것입니다. 드레싱 교체 준비 방법: 드레싱을 만지기 전에 손을 깨끗이 씻습니다. 필요한 모든 물품이 가까이에 있는지 확인합니다. 깨끗한 작업 공간을 확보합니다. 오래된 드레싱 제거 방법: 피부에서 테이프를 조심스럽게 떼어냅니다. 깨끗한(멸균이 아닌) 의료용 장갑을 사용하여 오래된 드레싱을 잡고 떼어냅니다. 드레싱이 상처에 붙어 있으면 적신 후 다시 시도합니다. 단, 담당 의료진이 마른 상태로 떼어내라고 지시한 경우는 예외입니다. 오래된 드레싱을 비닐봉지에 넣고 옆에 둡니다. 오래된 드레싱을 제거한 후 다시 손을 씻습니다. 상처 관리 상처 주변 피부를 닦기 위해 거즈 패드나 부드러운 천을 사용할 수 있습니다: 생리식염수(소금물) 또는 순한 비눗물을 사용합니다. 거즈나 천을 식염수 또는 비눗물에 적신 후, 피부를 부드럽게 두드리거나 닦아냅니다. 모든 분비물과 피부에 쌓인 마른 피 또는 기타 이물질을 제거합니다. 피부 세정제, 알코올, 과산화수소, 요오드, 또는 항균 화학물질이 함유된 비누를 사용하지 마십시오. 이러한 것들은 상처 조직을 손상시키고 치유를 늦출 수 있습니다. 담당 의료진이 상처를 세척하도록 요청할 수도 있습니다: 담당 의료진이 권장하는 대로 주사기에 소금물 또는 비눗물을 채웁니다. 상처에서 1~6인치(2.5~15센티미터) 떨어진 곳에서 주사기를 잡습니다. 분비물과 삼출물을 씻어낼 수 있을 정도로 세게 상처에 분사합니다. 깨끗하고 부드러운 마른 천이나 거즈 조각으로 상처를 조심스럽게 두드려 말립니다. 담당 의료진이 괜찮다고 말하지 않는 한, 상처 위나 주변에 로션, 크림, 또는 약초 치료제를 바르지 마십시오. 새 드레싱 붙이기 담당 의료진이 가르쳐 준 대로 상처에 깨끗한 드레싱을 붙입니다. 드레싱을 붙이기 전에 적셔야 하는 습건 드레싱을 사용하고 있을 수 있습니다. 끝나면 손을 깨끗이 씻습니다. 오래된 드레싱과 기타 사용한 물품은 방수 비닐봉지에 버립니다. 단단히 밀봉한 후 이중으로 봉한 다음 쓰레기통에 버립니다. 드레싱 교체 시 오염된 세탁물은 다른 세탁물과 분리하여 세탁합니다. 세탁물에 표백제를 첨가해야 하는지 담당 의료진에게 문의하십시오. 드레싱은 한 번만 사용하십시오. 절대 재사용하지 마십시오. 의사에게 연락해야 할 때 다음과 같은 경우 담당 의료진에게 연락하십시오: 상처 부위에 발적, 통증, 부종 또는 출혈이 증가한 경우. 상처가 더 커지거나 깊어지거나, 건조해 보이거나 어두워 보이는 경우. 상처에서 나오거나 주변의 분비물이 증가하거나, 걸쭉해지거나, 황갈색, 녹색 또는 노란색으로 변하거나, 악취가 나는 경우(고름이 있음을 나타냄). 체온이 100.5°F(38°C) 이상인 경우.

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. --- 고관절 치환술 - 퇴원 안내 고관절의 전부 또는 일부를 인공관절(프로스테시스, prosthesis)로 바꾸는 수술을 받았습니다. 이 글은 병원을 떠난 후 새로 바꾼 고관절을 어떻게 관리해야 하는지 알려줍니다. 병원에 있는 동안 고관절의 전부 또는 일부를 인공관절로 바꾸는 고관절 치환술을 받았습니다. 이 인공관절을 프로스테시스(prosthesis)라고 합니다. 집에서 기대할 수 있는 것 집에 갈 때쯤이면 보행기나 목발을 사용해 거의 도움 없이 걸을 수 있어야 합니다. 대부분의 사람들은 2~4주 후에는 보행기나 목발이 필요하지 않습니다. 목발을 언제까지 사용해야 하는지는 반드시 담당 정형외과 의사와 상의하십시오. 옷 입기는 약간의 도움만으로 할 수 있어야 하고, 침대나 의자에 스스로 오르내릴 수 있어야 합니다. 화장실도 큰 도움 없이 사용할 수 있어야 합니다. 수술 후 몇 달 동안은 특히 인공 고관절이 빠지지(탈구되지) 않도록 매우 조심해야 합니다. 새 고관절을 튼튼하게 하는 운동을 배우고, 특별한 주의사항을 지켜야 합니다. 퇴원 후 1~2주 동안은 집에 항상 누군가가 함께 있어야 합니다. 식사 준비, 목욕, 집 안에서 움직이기, 기타 일상생활을 도와줄 사람이 필요합니다. 시간이 지나면 이전과 비슷한 활동 수준으로 돌아갈 수 있어야 합니다. 하지만 내리막 스키(downhill skiing)나 축구, 미식축구 같은 격렬한 접촉 스포츠는 피해야 합니다. 대신 하이킹, 정원 가꾸기, 수영, 테니스, 골프 같은 관절에 무리가 덜 가는 운동은 할 수 있습니다. 집안 환경 정리 침대에 걸터앉았을 때 발이 바닥에 닿을 만큼 낮아야 합니다. 동시에, 침대에 앉았을 때 엉덩이가 무릎보다 높게 위치하도록 충분히 높아야 합니다. 병원용 침대는 꼭 필요하지 않지만, 매트리스는 단단한 것이 좋습니다. 집 안에서 넘어질 위험이 있는 것들을 치우십시오. 낙상을 예방하는 법을 배우십시오. 방에서 방으로 다닐 때 지나는 길에 있는 느슨한 전선이나 코드(줄)를 치우십시오. 미끄러지기 쉬운 작은 러그(깔개)는 치우십시오. 작은 반려동물은 집 안에 두지 마십시오. 문턱 주변의 바닥이 울퉁불퉁하면 고치십시오. 집 안 조명을 밝게 하십시오. 욕실을 더 안전하게 만드십시오. 욕조나 샤워실 안과 변기 옆에 손잡이(손잡이 난간)를 설치하십시오. 욕조나 샤워실 바닥에는 미끄럼 방지 매트를 깔아 두십시오. 집 안을 걸어 다닐 때는 아무것도 들고 다니지 마십시오. 균형을 잡기 위해 손을 써야 할 수 있습니다. 물건은 보행기나 목발에 주머니나 홀더를 달아 넣어 다니십시오. 물건은 쉽게 손이 닿는 곳에 두십시오. 등받이가 단단한 의자를 부엌, 침실, 욕실, 자주 사용하는 방에 두십시오. 그러면 일상적인 일을 할 때 앉아서 할 수 있습니다. 계단을 오르내리지 않아도 되도록 집안을 정리하십시오. 예를 들면: 1층에 침대를 두거나 1층 방을 침실로 사용하십시오. 하루 중 대부분의 시간을 보내는 층에 화장실이나 이동식 변기를 두십시오. 활동 걷기, 앉기, 눕기, 옷 입기, 목욕이나 샤워, 기타 활동을 할 때 새 고관절이 빠지지 않도록 조심해야 합니다. 낮은 의자나 푹신한 소파에 앉지 마십시오. 집에 돌아온 후에도 계속 움직이고 걷도록 하십시오. 한 번에 45분 이상 계속 앉아 있지 않도록 하십시오. 45분 이상 앉아 있어야 한다면, 45분마다 일어나서 잠시 움직이십시오. 담당 의사가 괜찮다고 말해 줄 때까지는 수술한 쪽 다리에 체중을 전부 싣지 마십시오. 처음에는 짧은 시간만 활동하고, 점차 활동 시간을 늘리십시오. 집에서 할 운동은 담당 정형외과 의사나 물리치료사가 알려줄 것입니다. 필요한 동안은 목발이나 보행기를 사용하십시오. 사용을 중단하기 전에는 반드시 담당 의사와 상의하십시오. 수술 후 며칠이 지나면 간단한 집안일은 할 수 있을 것입니다. 그러나 청소기 돌리기나 빨래 같은 무거운 집안일은 하지 마십시오. 처음에는 쉽게 피곤해진다는 것을 기억하십시오. 쪼그려 앉아 청소하지 마십시오. 작은 힙색(허리 가방)이나 배낭을 메거나, 보행기...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Replacement Surgery - Discharge Instructions You have had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint (prosthesis). This document tells you how to take care of your new hip after you leave the hospital. While you are in the hospital You have had hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to expect at home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches with little help. Most people do not need a walker or crutches after 2 to 4 weeks. Be sure to talk with your orthopedic surgeon about how long you need to use crutches. You should be able to get dressed with only a little help, and you should be able to get in and out of a bed or chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the bathroom without much help. For several months after surgery, you must be very careful so that the artificial hip does not come out (dislocate). You will learn exercises to strengthen your new hip and you must follow special precautions. For 1 to 2 weeks after discharge, someone should always be with you at home. You will need someone to help with preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to an activity level similar to before. However, you should avoid strenuous contact sports such as downhill skiing or soccer and American football. Instead, you can do exercises that put less strain on the joints, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, tennis, and golf. Preparing your home environment Your bed should be low enough that your feet touch the floor when you sit on the edge. At the same time, it should be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the bed. A hospital bed is not absolutely necessary, but the mattress should be firm. Remove anything in the home that could cause you to fall. Learn how to prevent falls...

You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. 고관절 치환술 - 퇴원 귀하는 고관절의 전체 또는 일부를 인공관절이라고 하는 보형물로 교체하는 수술을 받으셨습니다. 이 문서에서는 퇴원 후 새로운 고관절을 관리하기 위해 해야 할 사항을 알려드립니다. 병원에 있을 때 귀하는 고관절의 전체 또는 일부를 인공관절로 교체하는 고관절 치환술을 받으셨습니다. 이 인공관절을 보형물이라고 합니다. 퇴원 후 예상되는 사항 퇴원할 때쯤이면 많은 도움 없이 보행기나 목발을 사용하여 걸을 수 있어야 합니다. 대부분의 사람들은 2~4주 후에는 보행기나 목발이 필요하지 않습니다. 목발 사용을 언제 중단해야 하는지 담당 외과의사에게 확인하십시오. 또한 약간의 도움만 받으면 옷을 스스로 입을 수 있어야 하며, 침대나 의자에서 혼자 오르내릴 수 있어야 합니다. 화장실도 많은 도움 없이 사용할 수 있어야 합니다. 특히 수술 후 처음 몇 개월 동안은 인공 고관절이 탈구되지 않도록 주의해야 합니다. 새로운 고관절을 강화하는 운동을 배우고 특별한 주의사항을 지켜야 합니다. 병원이나 재활센터에서 퇴원한 후 1~2주 동안은 집에서 누군가와 함께 있어야 합니다. 식사 준비, 목욕, 집안 이동, 기타 일상 활동에 도움이 필요할 것입니다. 시간이 지나면 이전의 활동 수준으로 돌아갈 수 있어야 합니다. 스키나 축구, 미식축구와 같은 접촉 스포츠는 피해야 합니다. 그러나 하이킹, 정원 가꾸기, 수영, 테니스, 골프와 같은 저충격 활동은 할 수 있어야 합니다. 집 환경 준비 침대는 침대 가장자리에 앉았을 때 발이 바닥에 닿을 정도로 낮아야 합니다. 또한 침대 가장자리에 앉았을 때 엉덩이가 무릎보다 높도록 침대가 충분히 높아야 합니다. 병원용 침대는 필요하지 않을 수 있지만, 매트리스는 단단해야 합니다. 집에서 걸려 넘어질 위험 요소를 제거하십시오. 낙상 예방법을 배우십시오. 한 방에서 다른 방으로 이동하는 통로에서 느슨한 전선이나 줄을 치우십시오. 느슨한 깔개를 치우십시오. 작은 반려동물을 집에 두지 마십시오. 문턱의 고르지 않은 바닥을 수리하십시오. 충분한 조명을 사용하십시오. 욕실을 더 안전하게 만드십시오. 욕조나 샤워실, 변기 옆에 손잡이를 설치하십시오. 욕조나 샤워실에 미끄럼 방지 매트를 깔아두십시오. 걸어 다닐 때 아무것도 들고 다니지 마십시오. 균형을 잡기 위해 손이 필요할 수 있습니다. 대신 보행기나 목발에 주머니나 홀더를 달아 물건을 넣을 수 있습니다. 물건을 쉽게 닿을 수 있는 곳에 두십시오. 주방, 침실, 욕실 및 사용할 다른 방에 등받이가 단단한 의자를 두십시오. 이렇게 하면 일상적인 일을 할 때 앉을 수 있습니다. 계단을 오르내릴 필요가 없도록 집을 준비하십시오. 몇 가지 팁은 다음과 같습니다: 1층에 침대를 설치하거나 침실을 사용하십시오. 하루 중 대부분의 시간을 보내는 층에 욕실이나 이동식 변기를 두십시오. 활동 걷기, 앉기, 눕기, 옷 입기, 목욕이나 샤워, 기타 활동을 할 때 새 고관절이 탈구되지 않도록 주의해야 합니다. 낮은 의자나 푹신한 소파에 앉는 것을 피하십시오. 집에 돌아가면 계속 움직이고 걸으십시오. 한 번에 45분 이상 앉아 있지 않도록 하십시오. 더 오래 앉아 있어야 한다면 45분 후에 일어나서 움직이십시오. 외과의사가 괜찮다고 할 때까지 새 고관절이 있는 쪽에 전체 체중을 싣지 마십시오. 짧은 시간의 활동으로 시작한 다음 점차 늘려가십시오. 외과의사나 물리치료사가 집에서 할 수 있는 운동을 알려줄 것입니다. 필요한 만큼 목발이나 보행기를 사용하십시오. 사용을 중단하기 전에 외과의사에게 확인하십시오. 며칠 후에는 간단한 집안일을 할 수 있을 것입니다. 청소기 돌리기나 빨래와 같은 무거운 집안일은 하지 마십시오. 처음에는 쉽게 피곤해질 것입니다. 청소하려고 쪼그려 앉지 마십시오. 작은 허리 가방이나 배낭을 착용하거나, 보행기에 바구니나 튼튼한 가방을 달아서 전화기나 메모장과 같은 작은 생활용품을 가지고 다닐 수 있게 하십시오. 상처 관리 상처의 드레싱(붕대)을 깨끗하고 건조하게 유지하십시오. 외과의사가 알려준 시기에 드레싱을 교체할 수 있...

Hip Replacement - Discharge You have undergone surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint (hip joint) with an artificial joint (prosthesis). This article tells you what you need to do to manage your new hip joint after leaving the hospital. While in the hospital You received hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is also called a prosthesis. Life at home after discharge By the time you go home, you should be able to walk using a walker or crutches without much help. Most people do not need these after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon (the doctor who operated) about when to stop using crutches. Also, you should be able to dress with only a little help, and be able to sit and stand up from a bed or chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. Especially during the first few months after surgery, you must be careful so that the artificial hip joint does not dislocate (fall out). You must learn exercises to make the new hip joint strong and take special precautions. You should be with someone at home for 1 to 2 weeks after being discharged from the hospital or rehabilitation center. You need someone to help with meal preparation, bathing, moving around the house, and other daily activities. Over time, you will be able to return to your previous level of activity. You should avoid contact sports such as downhill skiing, American football, and soccer. However, you can do low-impact activities such as hiking, gardening, swimming, tennis, and golf. Setting up the home environment The bed should be low enough so that your feet touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Also, the bed should be high enough so that your hips are positioned higher than your knees when you sit on the edge of the bed. You will not need a hospital bed, but a firm mattress is good. Item: EVAL_0295 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: kimi-k2

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? Item: EVAL_0303 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: kimi-k2

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Changing the warfarin taking method, taking it together with other medication or at a different time, or eating specific food can change the way warfarin acts in the body. If such a thing occurs, the possibility that a blood clot forms or a bleeding problem occurs can become higher. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medication that lowers the possibility of blood forming blood clots. It can be important in the following cases: • If you already had a blood clot in the leg, arm, heart, or brain • If a medical provider is concerned that a blood clot can form in the body. People who have a new heart valve, enlarged heart, abnormal heart rhythm, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. While taking warfarin, the possibility of bleeding occurring can become higher even in activities you have usually been doing. Changing the way you take warfarin, taking other medication, or eating specific food can all change the way warfarin acts in the body. If such a thing occurs, the possibility that a blood clot forms or a bleeding problem occurs can become higher. Taking Warfarin It is important to take warfarin exactly as the doctor said. • Take only the dosage prescribed by the medical provider. If you forgot the dosage, contact the provider for advice. • If the medication looks different from the previous prescription, contact the provider or pharmacist immediately. Tablets have different colors according to dosage. The dosage is also indicated on the pill. • The medical provider will test blood during regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. This test is helpful in confirming whether you are taking the proper amount of warfarin that is helpful to the body. Original English:

Heart attack – discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Hypertension - Things to Ask a Doctor When the heart pumps blood into the arteries, the pressure of blood against the artery walls is called blood pressure. Blood pressure is displayed as two numbers: systolic blood pressure/diastolic blood pressure. Systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the heart beat cycle. Diastolic blood pressure is the lowest blood pressure. When blood pressure becomes too high, it gives additional burden to the heart and blood vessels. When blood pressure continues to be maintained high, the risk of heart attack and other blood vessel (blood vessel) diseases, stroke, kidney disease, and other health problems becomes higher. The following are questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider for blood pressure management. How much drinking is okay? * What can I do to quit smoking? Is it okay to be around other people who smoke? Should I measure blood pressure at home? * What kind of equipment should I purchase? Where can I learn how to use it? * How often should I measure blood pressure? Should I record it and bring it to the next appointment? * If I cannot measure blood pressure myself, where can I get it measured? * What should my blood pressure numbers be? Should I rest before measuring blood pressure? * When should I contact my healthcare provider? What are my cholesterol numbers? Should I take medication? Is it okay to have a sex life? What type of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I bring with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should I next have an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact my eye doctor about? Item: EVAL_0341 | Document: DIAB_002 | Model: kimi-k2

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. Back-Translation (to English):

Hypoglycemia - Self-management Hypoglycemia is a condition where blood sugar (glucose) occurs lower than normal. Hypoglycemia can occur in diabetes patients who take insulin or certain other medications to manage diabetes. Hypoglycemia can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize hypoglycemia symptoms and prevent them. What is hypoglycemia? Hypoglycemia is called hypoglycemia. If blood sugar level is below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L), it is hypoglycemia and can cause harm. If blood sugar level is below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L), immediate action is needed. Diabetes patients who take any one of the following diabetes medications are at risk for hypoglycemia: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) Even if you have had hypoglycemia before, the risk of hypoglycemia occurrence increases. Recognizing hypoglycemia You need to know how to notice that blood sugar is dropping. Symptoms include the following: Feeling weak or tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling anxious or nervous Feeling irritated Difficulty thinking clearly Overlapping or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes even if there are no symptoms, blood sugar can be too low. If blood sugar drops too low, the following can occur: Fainting Seizures Coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time become unable to detect hypoglycemia. This is called hypoglycemia unawareness. Ask your health care provider whether wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help detect when blood sugar is dropping too low and help prevent symptoms. Check your blood sugar frequently Consult with your provider about when to check your blood sugar every day. People who have hypoglycemia need to check their blood sugar more frequently. The most common causes of hypoglycemia are as follows: Administering insulin or diabetes medication at the wrong time Administering too much insulin or diabetes medication Administer...

Item: EVAL_0343 | Document: RESP_001 | Model: kimi-k2

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk around very much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save some of my energy around the house...

Because of this, it can become difficult to get enough oxygen and expel carbon dioxide from the lungs. There is no cure for COPD, but there are many things you can do to control symptoms and improve quality of life. The following are questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider to help manage your lungs. Questions What worsens COPD? How should you avoid things that worsen COPD? Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. --- --- Surgical wound management - Open type An incision is cutting the skin during surgery. This is also called a surgical wound. There are cases where the incision is small and cases where it is long. The size of the incision varies depending on the type of surgery you have received. Sometimes the incision site can open or split. This can occur across the entire incision or only in part. The surgeon in charge may decide not to close it again with sutures (thread). Expected situation at home If the surgeon does not close the wound again with sutures, recovery may take time, so you must manage it directly at home. The wound heals from bottom to top. Dressing helps absorb drainage and prevent the skin from closing before the bottom of the wound is filled. Proper hand washing It is important to wash hands cleanly before replacing dressing. You can use alcohol-based hand sanitizer. Or you can wash your hands according to the following steps: 1. Remove all jewelry from your hands. 2. With hands facing down, wet your hands under warm running water. 3. Apply soap and wash your hands for 15-30 seconds (while singing the Happy Birthday song or ABC song once). Clean under your fingernails as well. 4. Rinse cleanly. 5. Dry with a clean towel. Removing used dressing Medical staff will tell you how often you should replace dressing. To prepare for dressing replacement: - Wash your hands cleanly before touching dressing. - Prepare so that all necessary items can be easily obtained. - Prepare a clean work surface. Remove the used dressing: - Carefully peel off the tape from the skin. - Using clean (not sterile) medical gloves, grasp and remove the used dressing. - If the dressing is stuck to the wound, wet it with water and try again unless medical staff instructed you to peel it off dry. - Put the used dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside on one side. - Wash your hands cleanly again after removing the used dressing. Item: EVAL_0383 | Document: SURG_002 | Model: kimi-k2

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Hip Replacement Surgery - Discharge You have undergone surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis (prosthesis). This document provides guidance on how to care for your new hip joint after discharge. While in the hospital You have undergone hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. Expected situation at home By the time of discharge, you should be able to walk with almost no help using a walker or crutches. Most people will not need walking assistive devices after 2-4 weeks. Consult your surgeon about when to discontinue crutch use. Additionally, you should be able to dress yourself with only a little help, and get in and out of bed or chairs by yourself. You should also be able to use the bathroom by yourself with almost no help. Especially during the first few months after surgery, you must be careful to prevent dislocation of the artificial hip joint. You must learn exercises to make the new hip joint stronger and follow special precautions. For 1-2 weeks after leaving the hospital or rehabilitation center, you should have someone with you at home. You will need help with meal preparation, bathing, moving around the house, and other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your previous activity level. You should avoid contact sports like alpine skiing, American football, or soccer. However, you should be able to do low-impact activities like hiking, gardening, swimming, tennis, and golf. Preparing your home environment The bed should be low enough that your feet touch the floor when you sit on it. Additionally, it should be high enough that your hips are positioned higher than your knees when you sit on the bed. A hospital bed is not absolutely necessary, but the mattress should be firm.
